[3/11/25, 09:08:55] Haziq KXHL: Ok hereâ€™s the dealâ€¦ so we use box-cox cos we wanna use linear regression but the input-output relationship is kinda curvy yes?

But linear regression assumes additive + homoscedastic noise (not as fancy as it sounds, look it up). This no longer holds post transformation so thatâ€™s why this method is kind of ad-hoc and weâ€™re better off fitting a more flexible function to the untransformed data.

Like take the special case, lambda = 0 of box-cox and thatâ€™s just a log transform. A fixed error epsilon has a big effect in lower values of the input space but practically negligible at higher values. Think about what happens when you take log(1 + 0.1) vs. Log(10000 + 0.1). This is due to the â€œdiminishing returnsâ€ (everywhere monotonically increasing and negative 2nd derivative) property of the log function. But essentially your regression is gonna biased such that the model is more lenient towards errors at higher values of the input space which is like a really undesirable property â€<This message was edited>
[3/11/25, 09:14:08] Haziq KXHL: So box-cox is taught for historical reasons mostly, better off fitting a more flexible function (polynomial regressionâ€¦ ğŸ¤·â€â™‚ï¸) to data directly or using transformations that are faithful to the original noise

[7/12/25, 14:44:06] Ariel KXHL: I think we do need the assumptions to be true, if by true you mean â€œapply to the system youâ€™re modelingâ€. FEP makes certain assumptions about the system its modeling (e.g. constant Markov blanket). If these assumptions donâ€™t hold for the system youâ€™re modeling, you canâ€™t apply FEP. But if the assumptions hold, you can. 

Yes FEP is not opinionated about the particular form of the generative model, (you, as the scientist applying FEP, have to make that choice) but I think the fact that it provides any such framework at all for you to make the choices within is useful. There are plenty of active inference models of autism, psychiatric disorders, etc. that predict the data quite well. A particular application of FEP to some domain, with some specific generative model, can actually be falsified, though FEP itself cannot be (since itâ€™s a theorem that holds if you assume the assumptions it depends on are true)

And the particular assumptions that FEP makes does matter, because those assumptions dictate the scope of the theory; and I think the whole point is that the scope is very large. At its most ambitious it is trying to characterize (formally) what all systems that exist, that is maintain a boundary between themselves and the environment, must do

[10/12/25, 21:29:36] Ariel KXHL: Hi, good catch! I likely wonâ€™t respond to more stuff like this over whatsapp because your messages keep getting buried and Iâ€™m annoyed I have to keep scrolling up lmao. Email me or something if you have specific technical questions

Yes agree Helmholtz is way more general, but itâ€™s actually an iff. As in, f(x) = (Gamma - Q) blah blahâ€¦ <=> dp/dt = 0. The core of the proof is you note that dp/dt = 0 <=> divergence [ blah ] = 0 implies blah is divergence free, where blah is the relevant stuff in Fokker Planck. This is equivalent to saying blah is rewritable as Q nabla_x p(x), where Q is antisymmetric. And then you just rearrange some stuff, substitute in def of ln p(x) and you get the equivalence

If you actually want the details on the Helmholtz generalization you can dig through this somewhat impenetrable text https://nemenmanlab.org/~ilya/images/5/5e/Ao,_2004.pdf

[23/12/25, 20:55:58] Haziq KXHL: ah this is basically what they called NER in the old days â€œnamed entity recognitionâ€.
humans have strong priors over what looks like a name, transformers donâ€™t, so they treat â€œasdfasdfâ€ and â€œJames Deanâ€ equally.

in practice like ~98% of tokens in a paper are almost certainly not names. humans implicitly know this and skim. a vanilla transformer canâ€™t, so it computes full attention everywhere.

to reduce compute youâ€™d need a learned prior (e.g. an unsupervised or weakly supervised model over name-like strings) to gate or sparsify attention, so you only run full attention on the ~2% of tokens that are plausibly names (plus a small context window).

the prior doesnâ€™t solve the task, it just prunes the search space. thatâ€™s where the compute savings come from.
[23/12/25, 20:57:19] Yi Hein: canâ€™t u just add an attention penalty, so it learns those priors automatically?
[23/12/25, 20:58:43] Yi Hein: i am generally against handcrafting priors
[23/12/25, 20:59:14] Yi Hein: like the reason why i learn to look at at the structure of text / entity recognition is because iâ€™m too lazy to do the work of reading every single word
[23/12/25, 20:59:21] Yi Hein: my optimisation function is laziness
[23/12/25, 21:01:10] Haziq KXHL: you can add an attention penalty or regularisation, but that mostly just gives you a very generic prior, like â€œsimpler attention is betterâ€ in an Occamâ€™s razor sense.

that encourages sparsity, but it doesnâ€™t tell the model what to be sparse about. it still has to discover, at full cost, that names are special.

humans donâ€™t just have a simplicity prior. we have content specific inductive biases from experience, like what names usually look like and where they tend to appear in text.

if you actually want to add that bias, a probability model over name likeness is the straightforward way to do it. it lets you gate or sparsify attention before you pay the quadratic cost.

regularisation nudges behaviour. an explicit prior changes the search space.
[23/12/25, 21:02:48] Haziq KXHL: humans only do few-shot learning because we ship with hard-coded biases. face recognition is the classic example, thereâ€™s basically a proto-face template from birth. learning is fast because the search space is already constrained
[23/12/25, 21:03:17] Yi Hein: but how are those biases encoded though
[23/12/25, 21:03:25] Haziq KXHL: you already have a generative model for names right, just tie your NER to it

[23/12/25, 22:41:21] Yi Hein: Here is the translation of GÃ¶delâ€™s Ontological Argument into plain English.
Axiom 1
If a property is positive, and it necessarily implies a second property, then that second property is also positive.
Axiom 2
A property is positive if and only if its negation is not positive.
Theorem 1
If a property is positive, then it is possible that something exists that possesses it.
Definition 1
A being is defined as God-like if and only if it possesses every positive property.
Axiom 3
The property of being God-like is a positive property.
Theorem 2
It is possible that a God-like being exists.
Definition 2
A property is the essence of a being if:
 * The being holds that property, and
 * That property necessarily implies every other property the being holds.
Axiom 4
If a property is positive, then it is necessarily positive.
Theorem 3
If a being is God-like, then the property of being God-like is its essence.
Definition 3
A being has Necessary Existence if and only if the essence of that being is necessarily exemplified (meaning it is necessary that something with that essence exists).
Axiom 5
Necessary Existence is a positive property.
Theorem 4
Necessarily, a God-like being exists.

[2/1/26, 13:13:14] ~â€¯manav: I think coherent optimism is still possible but it requires carefully disentangling things. The only thing that needs to be treated as functionally transcendental is the quality of being a subject, i.e. a bare being-awareness to which objects appear. Anything that is an object is in principle empiricism-apt. All forms of mentation including self-awareness exist as objects in relation to the transcendental subject (you can observe your thoughts lol) so we can probably derive neural bases for these things and even speculate about non-biological substrates for mental activity. This way, by disambiguating the mind from awareness, we can 95% have our cake and eat it too, but the 5% is very important. What you cannot do is beg the question wrt self-referentiality Ã  la Hofstadterâ€™s strange loop, or think that you can empirically ground the very fact of experience itself. [9/1/26, 15:01:00] Haziq KXHL: i donâ€™t think evolution of evolvability works for two reasons

one// occamâ€™s razor. neo-wallacean evolution is already a minimal mechanism. evolvability adds a meta layer where selection somehow tunes the properties of mutation itself. thatâ€™s not something the theory predicts, thatâ€™s a rescue move

second/// it encodes intent. for evolvability to be selected, a lineage has to be fitter now because it will generate better variants later. but natural selection has no access to future fitness. neo-wallaceans are very explicit about this. selection only acts on present phenotypes. once you respect that constraint, most evolvability arguments collapse
[9/1/26, 15:01:59] Haziq KXHL: to elaborate on one...

[9/1/26, 15:37:30] Haziq KXHL: _*2//*_ the nicely biased operator problem. the moment you say evolution converges to a good search strategy

the space of desirable wells is massive, context dependent, environment specific and time varying. a bias that helps in one ecological regime will actively hurt you in another. so a search operator that is â€œnicely biasedâ€ in general is almost a contradiction. either itâ€™s strongly biased, in which case it overfits and gets stuck or itâ€™s weakly biased in which case it behaves basically like random search and youâ€™re back where you started (FLT / Free lunch theorem kills the goldilock's zone)

FLT angle: there is no universally good search strategy. any optimiser that works well is good because itâ€™s matched to a restricted class of problems. averaged over all landscapes, no strategy beats random search
[9/1/26, 15:39:11] Haziq KXHL: _*third//*_ the explanation still eats its own premise. the original reason to invoke evolvability is to explain how blind search gets anywhere at all. but now the explanation becomes: blind search works because it eventually discovers a really good way to not be blind anymore

ok, sick. how did it do that part

discovering a good search operator is itself a hard search problem. it requires new molecular machinery, new regulatory structure etc etc. and empirically we know how slow that kind of thing is. even something as trivial as a fluorescent protein takes hundreds of millions of years under basically unbiased search. so appealing to evolution discovering a good optimiser just pushes the difficulty up a level, it doesnâ€™t remove it

[15/1/26, 21:58:28] Haziq KXHL: i just wanna say why ppl cling so hard to monophyly / UCD (universal common descent) in OOL (origin of life) , bc abiogenesis is already doing an insane amount of work , like life out of literal chemistry soup , once is already a miracle we barely know how to tell a story about , so if u give up UCD u are basically forced to say ok cool this happened multiple times independently which is not just â€œharderâ€ but opens a massive explanatory gap

[15/1/26, 22:41:01] Haziq KXHL: ok so the genetic code being universal always gets used as one of the strongest arguments for UCD (universal common descent) right , like one code one ancestry etc

but if u actually sit with it for more than 5 seconds it kind of turns into a problem

bc there are only two times genetic code divergence could have happened , either before the genetic code of LUCA (last universal common ancestor) was fixed or after

before LUCA early life was communal as hell , HGT (horizontal gene transfer) everywhere , and if different lineages are using different codes then borrowed genes just turn into nonsense proteins , which kills HGT , which kills fast evolution , so multiple divergent codes cant really persist there

ok fine so what about after LUCA , after the code is fixed , well at that point translation is literally an operating system , even small changes break everything at once , proteins collapse globally , so organisms cannot actually explore fundamentally different codes there either

so divergence couldnt really happen before LUCA and it couldnt really happen after LUCA

and yet we observe more than 18 genetic codes ğŸ˜µâ€ğŸ’«

[16/1/26, 19:01:18] Haziq KXHL: hey @â¨~Anna Magpieâ© been reading kauffmanâ€™s origin of order stuff so hereâ€™s me doing due diligence bc i definitely handwaved it the first time lol

tl;dr NK model:
*N = number of components*
*K = number of interactions between components*

idea is NK defines the attractor landscape of some complex system
* high K = chaos, tons of attractors but search/navigation is cooked
* low K = smooth landscape, easy to navigate but basically no attractors
* medium K = â€œedge of chaosâ€, "structured complexity" kauffmanâ€™s sweet spot

so kauffmanâ€™s story is: darwinian evolution needs some ordered substrate (proto-life / simple replicator) and that substrate is supposed to be one of the attractor states of the prebiotic soup which he thinks naturally lives near edge-of-chaos K
[16/1/26, 19:05:03] Haziq KXHL: ok BUT thereâ€™s a tradeoff between *number of attractors* and *coherence of attractors*

if you want to say â€œyeah one of the attractors just happened to be proto-lifeâ€ that only really works if there are tons of attractors so itâ€™s not shocking that one is special

but kauffman explicitly doesnâ€™t like that regime bc thatâ€™s high K / chaos where attractors are too many, shallow and basically impossible to find & stabilise
[16/1/26, 19:05:59] Haziq KXHL: so instead he caps K at edge of chaos which gives you nice coherent attractorsâ€¦ but now you only have a small highly constrained set of attractors

and at that point itâ€™s no longer obvious at all why one of those few attractors should correspond to a proto-life state rather than just inert dynamical junk

so NK kinda forces you to choose:
* many attractors but no navigability (high K)
* navigability but too few attractors (edge of chaos)

and neither regime actually solves abiogenesis by â€œjust wait long enough and youâ€™ll hit lifeâ€
[16/1/26, 19:09:03] Haziq KXHL: ok so net net i think thereâ€™s only like two ways to crack abiogenesis and both are cursed

[20/1/26, 11:13:44] Haziq KXHL: ppl like to clown this idea bc itâ€™s not true but imo the real problem is itâ€™s acc a legit prediction of the framwork

[23/1/26, 19:12:47] ~â€¯Tuhan Sapumanage: Hey! Wanted to check I was right - basically we split encoding into two steps x -> x' -> y instead of x -> y (code for prior step is not in this snippet). What happens in x -> x' is giving each token local understanding of the context instead of doing all the permutations at once at x -> y. Computation is one reason but also avoids redundant learningâ€¦ So you learn token-to-token relationships first at x -> x' and then apply the nonlinear function to with that information stored in tokens at x' -> y.

tl;dr they are saying selection minimises KL divergence between the current allele distribution and the ESS (as selection does tbh, this is the fundamental premise in evo game theory)

interesting bit is they show this is equivalent to selection minimising "regret" (which is a concept from online machine learning which these AI acolytes probably know better than us) but basically it means selection converges optimally fast to the ESS and doesnt loiter in unfit states. very cool very cool

pivoting from there to saying selection acquires information is just machine learning brainrot bc acquiring information in the sense of minimising KL divergence to ESS is hardly useful in an origin-of-life context where the information problem is about functional information or algorithmic information (Kolmogorov complexity etc). not reshuffling probabilities over an existing allele set ğŸ™„

[25/1/26, 16:36:57] Haziq KXHL: @â¨Eli KXHLâ© hellllooooo hope this is Eli?? not 100% sure cos u dont have a display name but had a question for you since u work on the theoretical side of machine learning.

we were discussing regret functions from the online ML literature earlier and i was wondering why the process function / path integral formalisms from non equilibrium statistical mechanics havent really been used here especially when so many other analogies from physics are borrowed.

ofc the merit of any analogy comes from new insights or predictions that fall out of it. i remember us talking about how Le Cunâ€™s energy based formulation of deep learning is mostly aesthetic lol. but here is one thing that seems to fall out pretty immediately from regret x thermo.

in a closed thermodynamic system, there is a minimum energy dissipation cost incurred purely through entropy production (meaning irreversibility) even as the system tends towards a minimum free energy state.

by analogy, it seems like there should be a minimum regret cost incurred purely through the irreversibility of the learning process itself. this feels new because we usually think of regret as coming from bad proxy losses or bad data but this is due to entropy production of the learning dynamics itself. with aggressive optimisers,, the learning trajectory becomes irreversible due to sensitive dependence on initial conditions or Lyapunov type effects etc etc

[26/1/26, 13:15:18] Haziq KXHL: thing is we already have a pretty solid theory for this in the form of population coding and neural manifolds

this is maybe a bit niche and i can already feel the other neuro ppl in here side eyeing me lol but im kind of an acolyte. the idea is that the brain isnt representing all possible states explicitly, its using low dimensional structure embedded in high dimensional neural activity

so you get this huge apparent representational capacity without paying the full metabolic or combinatorial cost. the efficiency comes from geometry, correlations and circuit level constraints rather than exotic physics

which is why imo theres no real need to bring quantum into this particular parking lot fight when we already have good circuit level theories that explain both the efficiency and the flexibility pretty well

[28/1/26, 00:03:30] ~â€¯Ada Ge: Social capital is, in many ways, a leading indicator of financial capital, and so its nature bears greater scrutiny. Not only is it good investment or business practice, but analyzing social capital dynamics can help to explain all sorts of online behavior that would otherwise seem irrational.

In the past few years, much progress has been made analyzing Software as a Service (SaaS) businesses. Not as much has been made on social networks. Analysis of social networks still strikes me as being like economic growth theory long before Paul Romerâ€™s paper on endogenous technological change. However, we can start to demystify social networks if we also think of them as SaaS businesses, but instead of software, they provide status.



Can I use the social network to accumulate social capital? What forms? How is it measured? And how do I earn that status?



This is the classic cold start problem of social. The answer to the traditional chicken-and-egg question is actually answerable: what comes first is a single chicken, and then another chicken, and then another chicken, and so on. The harder version of the question is why the first chicken came and stayed when no other chickens were around, and why the others followed.
